Alright, let's consider potential correctness errors. 1. **Depthwise conv indices**: The kernel uses: `input[((n * C + c_in) * H + hh) * W + ww]`. In NCHW layout, offset for (n, c, h, w) is `((n*C + c)*H + h)*W + w`. This is correct. 2. **Weight indexing in depthwise**: `weight + c_in * 9`. The PyTorch conv weight is `[C, 1, 3, 3]`. Flattened as `[C*9]`. The first 9 elements correspond to c_in=0. So `weight[c_in*9 + kh*3 + kw]` is correct. 3. **Bias indexing**: We folded `bn` bias into `b_fold`. `b_fold` is shape `[C_out]` for conv and `[C]` for depthwise. So `bias[c_out]` and `bias[c_in]` are correct. 4. **Group conv weight indexing**: PyTorch group conv weight is `[C_out, C_in/groups, k, k]`. For 1x1, it's `[C_out, C_in_g, 1, 1]`. Viewing as `[C_out, C_in_g]`, the row for `c_out` is `weight[c_out*C_in_g + c]`. This matches our kernel: `const float* w_ptr = weight + (c_out * C_in_g);`. Correct. 5. **Group conv input indexing**: For group `g`, input channels are `g*C_in_g .. (g+1)*C_in_g - 1`. The base offset for channel `g*C_in_g + c` is `((n*C_in + g*C_in_g + c)*H + h)*W + w`. In our kernel, `inp_ptr = input + ((n * C_in + g * C_in_g) * HW + h * W + w)`. Then `inp_ptr[c * HW]` gives `input + ((n*C_in + g*C_in_g + c)*HW + h*W + w)`. Correct. 6. **Output indexing**: `output[((n * C_out + c_out) * H + h) * W + w]`. Correct. 7. **ReLU and Add**: `addend` uses same indexing as output. Correct. Everything seems consistent. But before that, I want to consider if we can optimize the 1x1 group conv further by having each thread compute multiple output channels? That would reduce the number of blocks and increase instruction-level parallelism. But our current design seems fine. Let's also consider using 16x16 thread blocks for the 1x1 conv instead of 1x56. Why does our design use `W` threads per block? Because we wanted to load weights into shared memory once per block (c_out). But we can load weights into shared memory for a block of output channels and have threads process multiple spatial locations. Actually, our current design has one block per (n, c_out, h), with W threads. So weights are loaded into SMEM once per (n, c_out, h). If weights are already in L1 cache, the SMEM load is fast. Can we increase work per block by having each thread process multiple h? E.g., blockDim = (W, TileH), and grid.x = N*C_out, grid.y = H/TileH. Then each block handles W*TileH spatial points for one (n, c_out). It loads the weight row once. This would reduce the number of times weights are loaded from global memory by a factor of TileH (since the weight row is loaded into SMEM once per block and shared across all threads). Currently, the weight row is loaded into SMEM once per (n, c_out, h). So for H=56, it's loaded 56 times per (n,c_out). If we have TileH=2, we load it 28 times. That halves the global memory reads for weights. Since weights are small but L1 cache might not retain them across different h blocks, this could help. But weight reading is tiny compared to input reading. The input is read `N*C_out*H*W*C_in_g` times. With our current kernel, each (n,c_out,h) block reads input for all c_in and w. The total input reads are the same regardless of block size. So the memory traffic is determined by the algorithm, not block size. However, having larger blocks may improve L1 hit rate for input because threads in the same block access overlapping input channels at the same time. But since each thread accesses a specific (h,w), and consecutive h blocks access different rows, larger TileH might improve row locality? Not really; each block accesses one row currently. If TileH=4, one block accesses 4 rows. The input for those rows might be cached. This is a micro-optimization. Let's stick to the simple design. But there's a more serious concern: `grid.y = H`. For stage3 with C_out=480, grid.x = 4800, grid.y=56. Total blocks = 268,800. H100 can handle millions of blocks concurrently? Actually, H100 has 132 SMs. Each SM can have up to 32 blocks. So total concurrent blocks is about 4224. With 268k blocks, it will serialize, but that's fine. The main issue is occupancy: each block has W=56 threads. 56 threads is less than a full warp (32). Actually, 56 threads = 2 warps (64 threads), with 8 threads idle in the second warp. So occupancy per block is low. But with many blocks, the SM can hold multiple blocks (up to 32). So 56 threads * 32 blocks = 1792 threads per SM. Max threads per SM is 2048. So occupancy is high (1792/2048 ≈ 87%). Good. Wait, let's check: H100 has max 32 blocks per SM and max 2048 threads per SM. With 56 threads per block, max blocks per SM is min(32, 2048/56 = 36) = 32. So 32 blocks * 56 = 1792 threads. Good. What if W=14 (not applicable here, but if it were)? Then 14 threads per block, min(32, 2048/14=146) = 32 blocks. 32*14=448 threads. Occupancy = 448/2048 = 22%. That's low. But W=56 here, so fine. Now, for the depthwise kernel, block = (16,16)=256 threads. min(32, 2048/256=8) = 8 blocks per SM. 8*256=2048 threads. 100% occupancy! Excellent. So the kernels have good occupancy. This is promising. Let's finalize the code. One small addition: In the C++ wrappers, we should check that the input is on CUDA and is float. ```cpp TORCH_CHECK(input.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "input must be float32"); ``` Similarly for other tensors. Also, weight for depthwise should have shape `[C, 9]`. We can check. ```cpp TORCH_CHECK(weight.dim() == 2 && weight.size(1) == 9, "depthwise weight must be [C,9]"); ``` For group conv weights: `weight.dim() == 2`. Check `C_in_g = C_in / groups`. Let's add these checks. Actually, `weight.size(0)` gives `C_out`, and `weight.size(1)` gives `C_in_g`. We can verify `C_in_g == input.size(1) / groups`. ```cpp TORCH_CHECK(input.size(1) % groups == 0, "C_in must be divisible by groups"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.size(1) == input.size(1) / groups, "weight size mismatch"); TORCH_CHECK(bias.size(0) == C_out, "bias size mismatch"); ``` I'll add these checks. reasoning
Let me fix the compilation issues: 1. C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK needs `#include <c10/cuda/CUDAGuard.h>` or similar - actually I can just remove it and use `__getLastCudaError` or simply remove these checks. 2. The functions need to be declared before the pybind statements - in load_inline, the functions should be defined in a header or the cuda code itself should be compiled first. Let me put declarations in cpp_sources. Actually for `load_inline`, the `cpp_sources` typically contains the C++ wrapper code, and `cuda_sources` contains the CUDA code. The problem is that the C++ files are compiled first and don't see the CUDA function declarations. I need to add forward declarations in `cpp_sources`. Let me also simplify - remove C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK and use a simpler error check, or just include the proper header. I'll structure it properly with cpp_sources containing forward declarations.

turn 4 · 1 tool call(s) · 38.9s · 19,992 chars (click to expand)
turn 4llm latency: 38.9s
reasoning
The CXXABI issue is an environment mismatch with the conda compiler vs system libstdc++. A workaround is to avoid needing compiled extensions for correctness checking / initial speedups. The main objective is just correct and faster than the reference. We could instead write pure PyTorch but fuse operations manually to reduce overhead and kernel launch counts; however, for a model this deep, Python overhead is significant and pure PyTorch cannot match a single-kernel fused approach. However, given the tool limitation, maybe we can use the `extra_cflags` to force linking with conda's libstdc++ or set `LD_LIBRARY_PATH`? Not possible. Another approach: compile with the system compiler instead of conda gcc. We can pass `extra_cflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0']` perhaps? No, that's compile-time. Another common trick on the cluster is to set env variable `LD_LIBRARY_PATH=/opt/conda/lib:$LD_LIBRARY_PATH`. Maybe we can do that inside the python script before loading the extension by modifying `os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH']` and then importing; but `load_inline` may already have done the loading? Wait, the compilation succeeds but import fails due to dynamic linker. We can set `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` before the import within the same process. However, the extension is loaded immediately upon `load_inline` returning. The import error happens during `load_inline` (it tries to import the .so). So we need to set the env var before `load_inline`. But `os.environ` modification before that should help? The process was already spawned; modifying `os.environ` does affect this process's environment for subsequent `dlopen` calls. So maybe setting `os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH'] = '/opt/conda/lib:' + os.environ.get('LD_LIBRARY_PATH','')` before `load_inline` will allow the dynamic linker to find the newer libstdc++. Let's try that.

But t2 has channels that were shuffled. For conv3, which is a group conv with `groups=3`, the input channels should be grouped according to the shuffled order. Since t2 was already shuffled by my depthwise kernel, the channel ordering is already shuffled. So when conv3 group g reads input channels [g*C_in_g, (g+1)*C_in_g), those are indeed the correct channels for group g because the shuffle changes the channel ordering but conv3's groups are defined on the shuffled ordering. Actually, wait. Let me re-examine this. In the reference model: After conv2 (depthwise), the channels are [0, 1, 2, ..., C-1]. After shuffle, they become [shuffle(0), shuffle(1), ...]. Then conv3 (group conv with groups=3) processes these shuffled channels. In my implementation: - The depthwise kernel writes the channels ALREADY SHUFFLED. So t2 has channels in shuffled order. - Then conv3 processes these shuffled channels. For conv3, group 0 reads the first C_in/3 channels of t2. In the reference, group 0 also reads the first C_in/3 channels after shuffle. Since t2 has the same channel ordering as the reference after shuffle, this should match. So I really don't see the mathematical error. Let me think about whether there could be an issue with `contiguous()`. Actually, wait. In the depthwise conv kernel, I use: ```c int C = input.size(1); // from the CPU side ``` And on the CPU side, I pass `C = input.size(1)`. For the depthwise conv, the weight has shape [C, 9]. But `C` is the number of channels in the input, which is also the number of channels in the output of conv2 (before shuffle). But the output of my depthwise kernel also has C channels (after shuffle, the count is the same). Wait, but what about the parameter `groups` in the depthwise kernel? I pass `unit.conv2.groups`, which is `mid_channels` (i.e., C). In the kernel, I compute: ```c int channels_per_group = C / groups; int group = c_out % groups; int local = c_out / groups; int c_in = group * channels_per_group + local; ``` When C = groups, channels_per_group = 1. group = c_out % C, local = c_out / C = 0 (for c_out < C). c_in = (c_out % C) * 1 + 0 = c_out. Wait, that's wrong! If C = groups, then: group = c_out % C = c_out (since c_out ranges from 0 to C-1) local = c_out / C = 0 c_in = group * 1 + 0 = c_out But for a proper channel shuffle with groups=3 and C=60: group = c_out % 3 local = c_out / 3 c_in = (c_out % 3) * 20 + (c_out / 3) I was passing `unit.conv2.groups` as the `groups` parameter, but that is `mid_channels`! For a depthwise conv, groups = mid_channels, not 3! So in my kernel, I was using `groups = mid_channels` instead of `groups = 3` for the shuffle operation. That's the bug! The shuffle should use the model's `groups=3`, not the depthwise conv's `groups=mid_channels`. Wait, let me verify. In the original ChannelShuffle: ```python class ChannelShuffle(nn.Module): def __init__(self, groups): self.groups = groups ``` And it's instantiated with `ChannelShuffle(groups)` where groups is passed from ShuffleNetUnit, which uses the model's groups=3. So in the unit: ```python self.shuffle = ChannelShuffle(groups) # groups=3 from constructor ``` And conv2 has groups=mid_channels (depthwise). So when I call my depthwise kernel, I should pass 3 (the shuffle groups), not `unit.conv2.groups` (which is mid_channels). This explains the bug! The depthwise kernel needs the shuffle groups=3, not the conv2 groups. Let me fix this.
